According to the [American Community Survey](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/American_Community_Survey "American Community Survey") (2020),<sup id="cite_ref-ACS2021_437-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-ACS2021-437"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>416<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> some 245.4 million people in the U.S. age five and older spoke only English at home. About 41.2 million spoke Spanish at home, making it the second most commonly used language. Other languages spoken at home by one million people or more include [Chinese](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chinese_language_in_the_United_States "Chinese language in the United States") (3.40 million), [Tagalog](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tagalog_language_in_the_United_States "Tagalog language in the United States") (1.71 million), [Vietnamese](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vietnamese_language_in_the_United_States "Vietnamese language in the United States") (1.52 million), [Arabic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabic_language_in_the_United_States "Arabic language in the United States") (1.39 million), [French](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/French_language_in_the_United_States "French language in the United States") (1.18 million), [Korean](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Korean_language_in_the_United_States "Korean language in the United States") (1.07 million), and [Russian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Russian_language_in_the_United_States "Russian language in the United States") (1.04 million). [German](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/German_language_in_the_United_States "German language in the United States"), spoken by 1 million people at home in 2010, fell to 857,000 total speakers in 2020.<sup id="cite_ref-438" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-438"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>417<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>

### Immigration

Main article: [Immigration to the United States](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Immigration_to_the_United_States "Immigration to the United States")

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Border_USA_Mexico.jpg/250px-Border_USA_Mexico.jpg)

The [Mexico–United States border wall](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mexico%E2%80%93United_States_border_wall "Mexico–United States border wall") between [San Diego](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/San_Diego "San Diego") (left) and [Tijuana](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tijuana "Tijuana") (right)

America's immigrant population is by far the world's [largest in absolute terms](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_sovereign_states_and_dependent_territories_by_immigrant_population "List of sovereign states and dependent territories by immigrant population").<sup id="cite_ref-UNdef_439-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-UNdef-439"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>418<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-440" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-440"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>419<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> In 2022, there were 87.7 million immigrants and [U.S.-born children of immigrants](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Second-generation_immigrants_in_the_United_States "Second-generation immigrants in the United States") in the United States, accounting for nearly 27% of the overall U.S. population.<sup id="cite_ref-441" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-441"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>420<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> In 2017, out of the U.S. foreign-born population, some 45% (20.7 million) were naturalized citizens, 27% (12.3 million) were lawful permanent residents, 6% (2.2 million) were temporary lawful residents, and 23% (10.5 million) were unauthorized immigrants.<sup id="cite_ref-KeyFindings_442-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-KeyFindings-442"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>421<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> In 2019, the top countries of origin for immigrants were Mexico (24% of immigrants), India (6%), China (5%), the Philippines (4.5%), and El Salvador (3%).<sup id="cite_ref-443" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-443"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>422<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> In fiscal year 2022, over one million immigrants (most of whom entered through [family reunification](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chain_migration#Legislation "Chain migration")) were granted [legal residence](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Permanent_residence_\(United_States\) "Permanent residence (United States)").<sup id="cite_ref-444" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-444"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>423<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> In fiscal year 2024 alone, according to the [Migration Policy Institute](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Migration_Policy_Institute "Migration Policy Institute"), the United States resettled 100,034 refugees, which "re-cements the United States' role as the top global resettlement destination, far surpassing other major resettlement countries in Europe and Canada".<sup id="cite_ref-445" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-445"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>424<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>

### Religion

Main article: [Religion in the United States](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Religion_in_the_United_States "Religion in the United States")

Religious affiliation in the U.S., according to a 2023 [Gallup](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gallup,_Inc. "Gallup, Inc.") poll:<sup id="cite_ref-Staff-2007_10-1" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-Staff-2007-10"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>

1. [Protestantism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Protestantism_in_the_United_States "Protestantism in the United States") (33%)
2. [Catholicism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Catholicism_in_the_United_States "Catholicism in the United States") (22%)
3. [Non-specific Christian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Christianity_in_the_United_States "Christianity in the United States") (11%)
4. [Judaism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Judaism_in_the_United_States "Judaism in the United States") (2%)
5. [Mormonism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mormonism_in_the_United_States "Mormonism in the United States") (1%)
6. Other religion (6%)
7. [Unaffiliated](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Irreligion_in_the_United_States "Irreligion in the United States") (22%)
8. Unanswered (3%)

The [First Amendment](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/First_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution "First Amendment to the United States Constitution") guarantees the [free exercise of religion in the country](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Free_Exercise_Clause "Free Exercise Clause") and forbids Congress from passing laws respecting [its establishment](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Establishment_Clause "Establishment Clause").<sup id="cite_ref-Donadio-2021_446-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-Donadio-2021-446"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>425<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-447" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-447"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>426<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> Religious practice is widespread, among the [most diverse](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_countries_by_ethnic_and_cultural_diversity_level "List of countries by ethnic and cultural diversity level") in the world,<sup id="cite_ref-alesina1_448-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-alesina1-448"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>427<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> and profoundly vibrant.<sup id="cite_ref-pewreligion_449-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-pewreligion-449"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>428<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> The country has the world's [largest Christian population](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Christianity_by_country "Christianity by country").<sup id="cite_ref-Global_Christianity_450-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-Global_Christianity-450"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>429<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> Other notable faiths include [Judaism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Judaism "Judaism"), [Buddhism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Buddhism "Buddhism"), [Hinduism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hinduism "Hinduism"), [Islam](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Islam "Islam"), many [New Age](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/New_Age "New Age") movements, and [Native American religions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Native_American_religions "Native American religions").<sup id="cite_ref-451" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-451"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>430<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> Religious practice varies significantly by region.<sup id="cite_ref-Williams-2023_452-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-Williams-2023-452"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>431<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> "[Ceremonial deism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ceremonial_deism "Ceremonial deism")" is common in American culture.<sup id="cite_ref-453" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-453"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>432<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>

The overwhelming majority of [Americans](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Americans "Americans") believe in a [higher power](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Higher_Power "Higher Power") or spiritual force, engage in [spiritual practices](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spiritual_practice "Spiritual practice") such as prayer, and consider themselves religious or [spiritual](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spirituality "Spirituality").<sup id="cite_ref-Kallo-2023_454-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-Kallo-2023-454"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>433<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-455" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-455"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>434<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> In the "[Bible Belt](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bible_Belt "Bible Belt")", located within the Southern United States, [evangelical Protestantism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evangelicalism "Evangelicalism") plays a significant role culturally, whereas [New England](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/New_England "New England") and the Western United States [tend to be more secular](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Unchurched_Belt "Unchurched Belt").<sup id="cite_ref-Williams-2023_452-1" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-Williams-2023-452"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>431<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> [Mormonism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mormonism "Mormonism")—a [Restorationist](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Restorationism "Restorationism") movement, whose members migrated westward from Missouri and Illinois under the leadership of [Brigham Young](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Brigham_Young "Brigham Young") in 1847 after the assassination of [Joseph Smith](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Joseph_Smith "Joseph Smith")<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHowe2008727–728_456-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHowe2008727%E2%80%93728-456"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>435<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>—remains the predominant religion in Utah to this day.

### Urbanization

Main articles: [Urbanization in the United States](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Urbanization_in_the_United_States "Urbanization in the United States") and [List of United States cities by population](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_United_States_cities_by_population "List of United States cities by population")

About 82% of Americans live in [urban areas](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States_urban_area "United States urban area"), including suburbs;<sup id="cite_ref-CIA-2018_194-3" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-CIA-2018-194"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>179<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> about half of those reside in cities with populations over 50,000.<sup id="cite_ref-457" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-457"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>436<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> In 2022, 333 [incorporated municipalities](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_United_States_cities_by_population "List of United States cities by population") had populations over 100,000, nine cities had more than one million residents, and four cities—[New York City](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/New_York_City "New York City"), [Los Angeles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Los_Angeles "Los Angeles"), [Chicago](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chicago "Chicago"), and [Houston](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Houston "Houston")—had populations exceeding two million.<sup id="cite_ref-458" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-458"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>437<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> Many U.S. metropolitan populations are growing rapidly, particularly in the South and West.<sup id="cite_ref-459" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-459"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>438<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>

### Health

Main articles: [Healthcare in the United States](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Healthcare_in_the_United_States "Healthcare in the United States"), [Healthcare reform in the United States](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Healthcare_reform_in_the_United_States "Healthcare reform in the United States"), and [Health insurance in the United States](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Health_insurance_in_the_United_States "Health insurance in the United States")

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Texas_medical_center.jpg/250px-Texas_medical_center.jpg)

The [Texas Medical Center](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Texas_Medical_Center "Texas Medical Center") in [Houston](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Houston "Houston") is the largest medical complex in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-461" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-461"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>440<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-462" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-462"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>441<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> In 2018, it employed 120,000 people and treated 10 million patients.<sup id="cite_ref-463" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-463"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>442<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>

According to the [Centers for Disease Control and Prevention](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Centers_for_Disease_Control_and_Prevention "Centers for Disease Control and Prevention") (CDC), average American life expectancy at birth was 78.4 years in 2023 (75.8 years for men and 81.1 years for women). This was a gain of 0.9 year from 77.5 years in 2022, and the CDC noted that the new average was largely driven by "decreases in mortality due to COVID-19, heart disease, unintentional injuries, cancer and diabetes".<sup id="cite_ref-464" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-464"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>443<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> Starting in 1998, life expectancy in the U.S. fell [behind that of other wealthy industrialized countries](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_countries_by_life_expectancy "List of countries by life expectancy"), and Americans' "health disadvantage" gap has been increasing ever since.<sup id="cite_ref-465" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-465"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>444<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>

The Commonwealth Fund reported in 2020 that the U.S. had the [highest suicide rate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_countries_by_suicide_rate "List of countries by suicide rate") among [high-income countries](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/High-income_countries "High-income countries").<sup id="cite_ref-466" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-466"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>445<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> [Approximately one-third of the U.S. adult population is obese](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Obesity_in_the_United_States "Obesity in the United States") and another third is overweight.<sup id="cite_ref-467" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-467"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>446<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> The U.S. healthcare system far [outspends that of any other country](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_countries_by_total_health_expenditure_\(PPP\)_per_capita "List of countries by total health expenditure (PPP) per capita"), measured both in per capita spending and as a percentage of GDP, but attains worse healthcare outcomes when compared to peer countries for reasons that are debated.<sup id="cite_ref-468" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-468"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>447<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> The United States is the only developed country [without a system of universal healthcare](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Healthcare_reform_in_the_United_States "Healthcare reform in the United States"), and [a significant proportion of the population that does not carry health insurance](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Health_insurance_coverage_in_the_United_States "Health insurance coverage in the United States").<sup id="cite_ref-469" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-469"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>448<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> Government-funded healthcare coverage for the poor ([Medicaid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Medicaid "Medicaid")) and for those age 65 and older ([Medicare](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Medicare_\(United_States\) "Medicare (United States)")) is available to Americans who meet the programs' income or age qualifications. In 2010, former President Obama passed the [Patient Protection and Affordable Care Act](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Patient_Protection_and_Affordable_Care_Act "Patient Protection and Affordable Care Act").<sup id="cite_ref-470" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-470"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>v<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-471" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-471"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>449<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> [Abortion in the United States](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abortion_in_the_United_States "Abortion in the United States") is not federally protected, and is illegal or restricted in 17 states.<sup id="cite_ref-472" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-472"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>450<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>

### Education

Main article: [Education in the United States](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Education_in_the_United_States "Education in the United States")

![Photograph of the University of Virginia](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/University-of-Virginia-Rotunda.jpg/250px-University-of-Virginia-Rotunda.jpg)

Some 77% of American college students attend public institutions<sup id="cite_ref-473" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-473"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>451<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> such as the [University of Virginia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/University_of_Virginia "University of Virginia"), founded by [Thomas Jefferson](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson "Thomas Jefferson") in 1819.

American primary and secondary education (known in the U.S. as [K–12](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/K%E2%80%9312_education_in_the_United_States "K–12 education in the United States"), "kindergarten through 12th grade") is decentralized. School systems are operated by state, territorial, and sometimes municipal governments and regulated by the [U.S. Department of Education](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Education "United States Department of Education"). In general, children are required to attend school or [an approved homeschool](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Homeschooling_in_the_United_States "Homeschooling in the United States") from the age of five or six ([kindergarten](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kindergarten "Kindergarten") or [first grade](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/First_grade "First grade")) until they are 18 years old. This often brings students through the [12th grade](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Twelfth_grade "Twelfth grade"), the final year of a U.S. high school, but some states and territories allow them to leave school earlier, at age 16 or 17.<sup id="cite_ref-474" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-474"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>452<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> The U.S. spends more on education per student than any other country,<sup id="cite_ref-475" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-475"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>453<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> an average of $18,614 per year per public elementary and secondary school student in 2020–2021.<sup id="cite_ref-476" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-476"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>454<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> Among Americans age 25 and older, 92.2% graduated from high school, 62.7% attended some college, 37.7% earned a [bachelor's degree](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bachelor%27s_degree "Bachelor's degree"), and 14.2% earned a graduate degree.<sup id="cite_ref-477" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-477"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>455<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> The [U.S. literacy rate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Literacy_in_the_United_States "Literacy in the United States") is near-universal.<sup id="cite_ref-CIA-2018_194-4" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-CIA-2018-194"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>179<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-478" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-478"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>456<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> The country has the [most Nobel Prize winners of any country](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_Nobel_laureates_by_country "List of Nobel laureates by country"), with [411](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_American_Nobel_laureates "List of American Nobel laureates") (having won 413 awards).<sup id="cite_ref-479" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-479"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>457<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-480" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-480"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>458<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>

[U.S. tertiary or higher education](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Higher_education_in_the_United_States "Higher education in the United States") has earned a global reputation. Many of the world's top universities, as listed by various ranking organizations, are in the United States, including 19 of the top 25.<sup id="cite_ref-481" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-481"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>459<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-482" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-482"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>460<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> American higher education is dominated by [state university systems](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/State_university_system "State university system"), although [the country's many private universities and colleges](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Private_universities_in_the_United_States "Private universities in the United States") enroll about 20% of all American students. Local [community colleges](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Community_college "Community college") generally offer coursework and degree programs covering the first two years of college study. They often have more open admission policies, shorter academic programs, and lower tuition.<sup id="cite_ref-483" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-483"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>461<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>

As for [public expenditures](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Public_expenditure "Public expenditure") on higher education, the U.S. spends more per student than the [OECD](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/OECD "OECD") average, and Americans spend more than all nations in combined public and private spending.<sup id="cite_ref-education_spending_484-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-education_spending-484"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>462<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> Colleges and universities directly funded by the federal government do not charge tuition and are limited to military personnel and government employees, including: the [U.S. service academies](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States_service_academies "United States service academies"), the [Naval Postgraduate School](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Naval_Postgraduate_School "Naval Postgraduate School"), and [military staff colleges](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/US_military_staff_colleges "US military staff colleges"). Despite some student [loan forgiveness](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Loan_forgiveness "Loan forgiveness") programs in place,<sup id="cite_ref-485" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-485"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>463<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> [student loan debt](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Student_debt "Student debt") increased by 102% between 2010 and 2020,<sup id="cite_ref-486" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-486"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>464<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> and exceeded $1.7 trillion in 2022.<sup id="cite_ref-487" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-487"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>465<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>

## Culture and society

Main articles: [Culture of the United States](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Culture_of_the_United_States "Culture of the United States"), [Society of the United States](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Society_of_the_United_States "Society of the United States"), and [Human rights in the United States](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_rights_in_the_United_States "Human rights in the United States")

![The Statue of Liberty, a large teal bronze sculpture on a stone pedestal](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Liberty02.jpg/250px-Liberty02.jpg)

The [Statue of Liberty](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Statue_of_Liberty "Statue of Liberty") (*Liberty Enlightening the World*) on [Liberty Island](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Liberty_Island "Liberty Island") in [New York Harbor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/New_York_Harbor "New York Harbor") was an 1866 gift from France that has become an iconic symbol of the [American Dream](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/American_Dream "American Dream").<sup id="cite_ref-488" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-488"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>466<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>

Americans have traditionally [been characterized](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stereotypes_of_Americans "Stereotypes of Americans") by a unifying political belief in an "[American Creed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/American_Creed "American Creed")" emphasizing [consent of the governed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Consent_of_the_governed "Consent of the governed"), [liberty](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Liberty "Liberty"), [equality under the law](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Equality_under_the_law "Equality under the law"), [democracy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Democracy "Democracy"), [social equality](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Social_equality "Social equality"), [property rights](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Property_rights "Property rights"), and a preference for [limited government](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Limited_government "Limited government").<sup id="cite_ref-489" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-489"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>467<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-490" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-490"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>468<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> Culturally, the country has been described as having [the values](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Americanism_\(ideology\) "Americanism (ideology)") of [individualism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Individualism "Individualism") and [personal autonomy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Left-libertarianism "Left-libertarianism"),<sup id="cite_ref-491" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-491"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>469<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-492" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-492"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>470<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> as well as having a strong [work ethic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Work_ethic "Work ethic"),<sup id="cite_ref-493" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-493"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>471<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> [competitiveness](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Competition "Competition"),<sup id="cite_ref-494" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-494"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>472<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> and voluntary [altruism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Altruism "Altruism") towards others.<sup id="cite_ref-495" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-495"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>473<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-496" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-496"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>474<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-497" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-497"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>475<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> According to a 2016 study by the [Charities Aid Foundation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Charities_Aid_Foundation "Charities Aid Foundation"), Americans donated 1.44% of total GDP to charity—the [highest rate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_countries_by_charitable_donation "List of countries by charitable donation") in the world by a large margin.<sup id="cite_ref-498" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-498"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>476<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> The United States is home to a [wide variety of ethnic groups, traditions, and values](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Multiculturalism "Multiculturalism").<sup id="cite_ref-499" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-499"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>477<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-500" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-500"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>478<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> The country has acquired significant [hard](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hard_power "Hard power") and [soft power](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soft_power "Soft power") through [its diplomatic influence](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Foreign_relations_of_the_United_States "Foreign relations of the United States"), [economic power](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Economic_power "Economic power"), [military alliances](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Military_alliance "Military alliance"), and [cultural exports](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cultural_exports "Cultural exports") such as [American movies](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cinema_of_the_United_States "Cinema of the United States"), [music](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Music_of_the_United_States "Music of the United States"), [video games](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Video_games_in_the_United_States "Video games in the United States"), [sports](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sports_in_the_United_States "Sports in the United States"), and [food](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/American_cuisine "American cuisine").<sup id="cite_ref-501" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-501"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>479<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-502" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-502"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>480<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> The influence that the United States exerts on other countries through soft power is referred to as [Americanization](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Americanization "Americanization").<sup id="cite_ref-503" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-503"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>481<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>

Nearly all present Americans or their ancestors came from [Europe, Africa, or Asia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Afro-Eurasia "Afro-Eurasia") (the "[Old World](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Old_World "Old World")") within the past five centuries.<sup id="cite_ref-504" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-504"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>482<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> [Mainstream](https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/mainstream "wikt:mainstream") American culture is a [Western culture](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Western_culture "Western culture") largely derived from the [traditions of European immigrants](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/European_American#Culture "European American") with influences from many other sources, such as [traditions brought by slaves from Africa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/African-American_culture "African-American culture").<sup id="cite_ref-505" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-505"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>483<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> More recent immigration from [Asia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Asian_American "Asian American") and especially [Latin America](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Latin_American_culture "Latin American culture") has added to a cultural mix that has been described as a homogenizing [melting pot](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Melting_pot "Melting pot"), and a heterogeneous [salad bowl](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Salad_bowl_\(cultural_idea\) "Salad bowl (cultural idea)"), with immigrants contributing to, and often [assimilating](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Assimilation_\(phonology\) "Assimilation (phonology)") into, mainstream American culture. The [American Dream](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/American_Dream "American Dream"), or the perception that Americans enjoy high [social mobility](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Socio-economic_mobility_in_the_United_States "Socio-economic mobility in the United States"), plays a key role in attracting immigrants.<sup id="cite_ref-506" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-506"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>484<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-507" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-507"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>485<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> Whether this perception is accurate has been a topic of debate.<sup id="cite_ref-socialmobility_508-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-socialmobility-508"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>486<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CAP_509-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-CAP-509"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>487<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-510" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-510"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>488<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> While mainstream culture holds that the United States is a [classless society](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Classless_society "Classless society"),<sup id="cite_ref-511" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-511"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>489<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> scholars identify significant differences between [the country's social classes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Social_class_in_the_United_States "Social class in the United States"), affecting [socialization](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Socialization "Socialization"), language, and values.<sup id="cite_ref-512" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-512"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>490<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-513" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-513"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>491<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> Americans tend to greatly value [socioeconomic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Socioeconomics "Socioeconomics") achievement, but [being ordinary or average](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Average_Joe "Average Joe") is promoted by some as a noble condition as well.<sup id="cite_ref-514" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-514"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>492<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>

The [National Foundation on the Arts and the Humanities](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_Foundation_on_the_Arts_and_the_Humanities "National Foundation on the Arts and the Humanities") is an agency of the United States federal government that was established in 1965 with the purpose to "develop and promote a broadly conceived national policy of support for the humanities and the arts in the United States, and for institutions which preserve the cultural heritage of the United States."<sup id="cite_ref-515" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-515"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>493<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> It is composed of four sub-agencies:

- [National Endowment for the Arts](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_Endowment_for_the_Arts "National Endowment for the Arts")
- [National Endowment for the Humanities](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_Endowment_for_the_Humanities "National Endowment for the Humanities")
- [Institute of Museum and Library Services](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Institute_of_Museum_and_Library_Services "Institute of Museum and Library Services")
- [Federal Council on the Arts and the Humanities](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Federal_Council_on_the_Arts_and_the_Humanities "Federal Council on the Arts and the Humanities")

The United States is considered to have the [strongest protections of free speech of any country](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Freedom_of_speech_in_the_United_States "Freedom of speech in the United States") under the [First Amendment](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/First_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution "First Amendment to the United States Constitution"),<sup id="cite_ref-Coleman-2013_516-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-Coleman-2013-516"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>494<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> which protects [flag desecration](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Flag_desecration_in_the_United_States "Flag desecration in the United States"), [hate speech](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hate_speech_in_the_United_States "Hate speech in the United States"), [blasphemy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Blasphemy_law_in_the_United_States "Blasphemy law in the United States"), and [lese-majesty](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/L%C3%A8se-majest%C3%A9 "Lèse-majesté") as forms of protected expression.<sup id="cite_ref-517" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-517"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>495<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-518" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-518"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>496<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-519" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-519"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>497<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> A 2016 [Pew Research Center](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pew_Research_Center "Pew Research Center") poll found that Americans were the most supportive of free expression of any polity measured.<sup id="cite_ref-520" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-520"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>498<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> They are the "most supportive of [freedom of the press](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Freedom_of_the_press_in_the_United_States "Freedom of the press in the United States") and the [right to use the Internet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Right_to_Internet_access "Right to Internet access") without government censorship".<sup id="cite_ref-521" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-521"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>499<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> The U.S. is a [socially progressive](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cultural_liberalism "Cultural liberalism") country<sup id="cite_ref-522" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-522"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>500<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> with [permissive](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Permissive_society "Permissive society") attitudes surrounding [human sexuality](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_sexuality "Human sexuality").<sup id="cite_ref-Derks-2020_523-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-Derks-2020-523"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>501<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> [LGBT rights in the United States](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/LGBT_rights_in_the_United_States "LGBT rights in the United States") are advanced by global standards.<sup id="cite_ref-Derks-2020_523-1" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-Derks-2020-523"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>501<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-524" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-524"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>502<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-525" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-525"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>503<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>

### Literature

Main articles: [American literature](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/American_literature "American literature") and [American philosophy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/American_philosophy "American philosophy")

![Photograph of Mark Twain](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Mark_Twain_by_AF_Bradley.jpg/250px-Mark_Twain_by_AF_Bradley.jpg)

[Mark Twain](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mark_Twain "Mark Twain"), whom [William Faulkner](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/William_Faulkner "William Faulkner") called "the father of American literature"<sup id="cite_ref-faulkner_526-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-faulkner-526"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>504<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>

Colonial American authors were influenced by [John Locke](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Locke "John Locke") and various other [Enlightenment](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment "Age of Enlightenment") philosophers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaymLevine2013157–159_527-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaymLevine2013157%E2%80%93159-527"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>505<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELauter1994a503–509_528-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-FOOTNOTELauter1994a503%E2%80%93509-528"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>506<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> The [American Revolutionary Period](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/American_Revolution "American Revolution") (1765–1783) is notable for the political writings of [Benjamin Franklin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Benjamin_Franklin "Benjamin Franklin"), [Alexander Hamilton](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_Hamilton "Alexander Hamilton"), [Thomas Paine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thomas_Paine "Thomas Paine"), and [Thomas Jefferson](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson "Thomas Jefferson"). Shortly before and after the [Revolutionary War](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/American_Revolution_War "American Revolution War"), the newspaper rose to prominence, filling a demand for anti-British national literature.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaymLevine2013163_529-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaymLevine2013163-529"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>507<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-530" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-530"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>508<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> An early novel is [William Hill Brown](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/William_Hill_Brown "William Hill Brown")'s *[The Power of Sympathy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Power_of_Sympathy "The Power of Sympathy")*, published in 1791. Writer and critic [John Neal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Neal "John Neal") in the early- to mid-nineteenth century helped advance America toward a unique literature and culture by criticizing predecessors such as [Washington Irving](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Washington_Irving "Washington Irving") for imitating their British counterparts, and by influencing writers such as [Edgar Allan Poe](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Edgar_Allan_Poe "Edgar Allan Poe"),<sup id="cite_ref-531" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-531"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>509<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> who took American poetry and short fiction in new directions. [Ralph Waldo Emerson](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ralph_Waldo_Emerson "Ralph Waldo Emerson") and [Margaret Fuller](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Margaret_Fuller "Margaret Fuller") pioneered the influential [Transcendentalism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Transcendentalism "Transcendentalism") movement;<sup id="cite_ref-532" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-532"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>510<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Coviello_533-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-Coviello-533"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>511<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> [Henry David Thoreau](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Henry_David_Thoreau "Henry David Thoreau"), author of *[Walden](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Walden "Walden")*, was influenced by this movement.

The conflict surrounding [abolitionism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abolitionism_in_the_United_States "Abolitionism in the United States") inspired writers, like [Harriet Beecher Stowe](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Harriet_Beecher_Stowe "Harriet Beecher Stowe"), and authors of slave narratives, such as [Frederick Douglass](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Frederick_Douglass "Frederick Douglass"). [Nathaniel Hawthorne](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nathaniel_Hawthorne "Nathaniel Hawthorne")'s *[The Scarlet Letter](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Scarlet_Letter "The Scarlet Letter")* (1850) explored the dark side of American history, as did [Herman Melville](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Herman_Melville "Herman Melville")'s *[Moby-Dick](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby-Dick "Moby-Dick")* (1851). Major American poets of the nineteenth century [American Renaissance](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/American_Renaissance_\(literature\) "American Renaissance (literature)") include [Walt Whitman](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Walt_Whitman "Walt Whitman"), Melville, and [Emily Dickinson](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Emily_Dickinson "Emily Dickinson").<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaymLevine2013444–447_534-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaymLevine2013444%E2%80%93447-534"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>512<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELauter1994a1228,_1233,_1260_535-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-FOOTNOTELauter1994a1228,_1233,_1260-535"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>513<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> [Mark Twain](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mark_Twain "Mark Twain") was the first major American writer to be born in the West. [Henry James](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Henry_James "Henry James") achieved international recognition with novels like *[The Portrait of a Lady](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Portrait_of_a_Lady "The Portrait of a Lady")* (1881). As literacy rates rose, periodicals published more stories centered around industrial workers, women, and the rural poor.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaymLevine20131269–1270_536-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaymLevine20131269%E2%80%931270-536"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>514<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELauter1994b8–10_537-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-FOOTNOTELauter1994b8%E2%80%9310-537"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>515<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> [Naturalism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Naturalism_\(literature\) "Naturalism (literature)"), [regionalism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/American_literary_regionalism "American literary regionalism"), and [realism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Literary_realism#UnitedStates "Literary realism") were the major literary movements of the period.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaymLevine20131271–1273_538-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaymLevine20131271%E2%80%931273-538"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>516<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELauter1994b12_539-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-FOOTNOTELauter1994b12-539"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>517<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>

While [modernism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Literary_modernism "Literary modernism") generally took on an international character, modernist authors working within the United States more often rooted their work in specific regions, peoples, and cultures.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaymLevine20131850–1851_540-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaymLevine20131850%E2%80%931851-540"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>518<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> Following the Great Migration to northern cities, African-American and black [West Indian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/West_Indian_Americans "West Indian Americans") authors of the [Harlem Renaissance](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Harlem_Renaissance "Harlem Renaissance") developed an independent tradition of literature that rebuked a history of inequality and celebrated black culture. An important cultural export during the [Jazz Age](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jazz_Age "Jazz Age"), these writings were a key influence on *[Négritude](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/N%C3%A9gritude "Négritude")*, a philosophy emerging in the 1930s among francophone writers of the [African diaspora](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/African_diaspora "African diaspora").<sup id="cite_ref-541" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-541"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>519<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-542" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-542"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>520<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> In the 1950s, an ideal of homogeneity led many authors to attempt to write the [Great American Novel](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Great_American_Novel "Great American Novel"),<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaymLevine20132260–2261_543-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaymLevine20132260%E2%80%932261-543"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>521<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> while the [Beat Generation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Beat_Generation "Beat Generation") rejected this conformity, using styles that elevated the impact of the [spoken word](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spoken_word "Spoken word") over mechanics to describe drug use, sexuality, and the failings of society.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaymLevine20132262_544-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaymLevine20132262-544"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>522<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-545" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-545"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>523<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> Contemporary literature is more pluralistic than in previous eras, with the closest thing to a unifying feature being a trend toward self-conscious [experiments with language](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Experimental_literature "Experimental literature").<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaymLevine20132266–2267_546-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaymLevine20132266%E2%80%932267-546"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>524<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> Twelve American laureates have won the [Nobel Prize in Literature](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Literature "Nobel Prize in Literature").<sup id="cite_ref-547" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-547"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>525<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>

### Mass media

Main article: [Mass media in the United States](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mass_media_in_the_United_States "Mass media in the United States")

See also: [Newspapers in the United States](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Newspapers_in_the_United_States "Newspapers in the United States"), [Television in the United States](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Television_in_the_United_States "Television in the United States"), [Broadcasting in the United States](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Broadcasting_in_the_United_States "Broadcasting in the United States"), [Public broadcasting in the United States](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Public_broadcasting_in_the_United_States "Public broadcasting in the United States"), [Internet in the United States](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Internet_in_the_United_States "Internet in the United States"), [Radio in the United States](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radio_in_the_United_States "Radio in the United States"), and [Video games in the United States](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Video_games_in_the_United_States "Video games in the United States")

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Comcastcenter_vertical.jpg/250px-Comcastcenter_vertical.jpg)

[Comcast Center](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Comcast_Center "Comcast Center") in [Philadelphia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Philadelphia "Philadelphia"), headquarters of [Comcast](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Comcast "Comcast"), one of the world's [largest telecommunications companies](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_telecommunications_companies "List of telecommunications companies") and media conglomerates

Media in the United States is [broadly uncensored](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Censorship_in_the_United_States "Censorship in the United States"), with the [First Amendment](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/First_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution "First Amendment to the United States Constitution") providing significant protections, as reiterated in *[New York Times Co. v. United States](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/New_York_Times_Co._v._United_States "New York Times Co. v. United States")*.<sup id="cite_ref-Coleman-2013_516-1" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-Coleman-2013-516"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>494<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> The four major broadcasters in the U.S. are the [National Broadcasting Company](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_Broadcasting_Company "National Broadcasting Company") (NBC), [Columbia Broadcasting System](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Columbia_Broadcasting_System "Columbia Broadcasting System") (CBS), [American Broadcasting Company](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/American_Broadcasting_Company "American Broadcasting Company") (ABC), and [Fox Broadcasting Company](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fox_Broadcasting_Company "Fox Broadcasting Company") (FOX). The four major broadcast television networks are all commercial entities. [Cable television](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cable_television_in_the_United_States "Cable television in the United States") offers hundreds of channels catering to a variety of niches.<sup id="cite_ref-548" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-548"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>526<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> In 2021, about 83% of Americans over age 12 listened to [broadcast radio](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radio_broadcasting "Radio broadcasting"), while about 40% listened to [podcasts](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Podcast "Podcast").<sup id="cite_ref-549" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-549"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>527<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> In the prior year, there were 15,460 licensed full-power radio stations in the U.S. according to the [Federal Communications Commission](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Federal_Communications_Commission "Federal Communications Commission") (FCC).<sup id="cite_ref-550" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-550"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>528<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> Much of the public radio broadcasting is supplied by [NPR](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NPR "NPR"), incorporated in February 1970 under the [Public Broadcasting Act of 1967](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Public_Broadcasting_Act_of_1967 "Public Broadcasting Act of 1967").<sup id="cite_ref-551" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-551"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>529<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>

U.S. newspapers with a global reach and reputation include *[The Wall Street Journal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Wall_Street_Journal "The Wall Street Journal")*, *[The New York Times](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_New_York_Times "The New York Times")*, *[The Washington Post](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Washington_Post "The Washington Post")*, and *[USA Today](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/USA_Today "USA Today")*.<sup id="cite_ref-Shaffer2006_552-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-Shaffer2006-552"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>530<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> [About 800 publications](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_Spanish-language_newspapers_published_in_the_United_States "List of Spanish-language newspapers published in the United States") are produced in Spanish.<sup id="cite_ref-553" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-553"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>531<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-554" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-554"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>532<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> With few exceptions, newspapers are privately owned, either by large chains such as [Gannett](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gannett_Company "Gannett Company") or [McClatchy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_McClatchy_Company "The McClatchy Company"), which own dozens or even hundreds of newspapers; by small chains that own a handful of papers; or, in an increasingly rare situation, by individuals or families. Major cities often have [alternative newspapers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alternative_newspaper "Alternative newspaper") to complement the mainstream daily papers, such as *[The Village Voice](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Village_Voice "The Village Voice")* in New York City and *[LA Weekly](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/LA_Weekly "LA Weekly")* in Los Angeles. The five most popular websites used in the U.S. are [Google](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Google_Search "Google Search"), [YouTube](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/YouTube "YouTube"), [Amazon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Amazon_\(website\) "Amazon (website)"), [Yahoo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yahoo "Yahoo"), and [Facebook](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Facebook "Facebook")—all of them American-owned.<sup id="cite_ref-alexa-topsitesus_555-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-alexa-topsitesus-555"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>533<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>

In 2022, the video game market of the United States was the world's [largest by revenue](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_video_games_markets_by_country "List of video games markets by country").<sup id="cite_ref-556" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-556"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>534<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> There are 444 publishers, developers, and hardware companies in California alone.<sup id="cite_ref-557" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-557"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>535<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>

### Theater

Main article: [Theater in the United States](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Theater_in_the_United_States "Theater in the United States")

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Broadway_Theaters_45th_Street_Night.jpg/250px-Broadway_Theaters_45th_Street_Night.jpg)

[Broadway theaters](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Broadway_theater "Broadway theater") in [Theater District, Manhattan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Theater_District,_Manhattan "Theater District, Manhattan")

The United States is well known for its theater. Mainstream theater in the United States derives from the old European theatrical tradition and has been heavily influenced by the [British theater](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Theatre_of_the_United_Kingdom "Theatre of the United Kingdom").<sup id="cite_ref-Saxon2011_558-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-Saxon2011-558"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>536<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> By the middle of the 19th century America had created new distinct dramatic forms in the [Tom Shows](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tom_Shows "Tom Shows"), the [showboat theater](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Showboat "Showboat") and the [minstrel show](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Minstrel_show "Minstrel show").<sup id="cite_ref-559" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-559"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>537<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> The central hub of the American theater scene is the [Theater District in Manhattan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Theater_District,_Manhattan "Theater District, Manhattan"), with its divisions of [Broadway](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Broadway_theatre "Broadway theatre"), [off-Broadway](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Off-Broadway "Off-Broadway"), and [off-off-Broadway](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Off-off-Broadway "Off-off-Broadway").<sup id="cite_ref-LondréWatermeier1998_560-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-Londr%C3%A9Watermeier1998-560"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>538<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>

Many movie and television [celebrities](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Celebrity "Celebrity") have gotten their big break working in New York productions. Outside New York City, many cities have professional [regional or resident theater companies](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Regional_theater_in_the_United_States "Regional theater in the United States") that produce their own seasons. The biggest-budget theatrical productions are musicals. U.S. theater has an active [community theater](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Community_theater "Community theater") culture.<sup id="cite_ref-561" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-561"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>539<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>

The [Tony Awards](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tony_Awards "Tony Awards") recognizes excellence in live Broadway theater and are presented at an annual ceremony in [Manhattan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Manhattan "Manhattan"). The awards are given for Broadway productions and performances. One is also given for [regional theater](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Regional_theatre_in_the_United_States "Regional theatre in the United States"). Several discretionary non-competitive awards are given as well, including a [Special Tony Award](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Special_Tony_Award "Special Tony Award"), the [Tony Honors for Excellence in Theatre](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tony_Honors_for_Excellence_in_Theatre "Tony Honors for Excellence in Theatre"), and the [Isabelle Stevenson Award](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Isabelle_Stevenson_Award "Isabelle Stevenson Award").<sup id="cite_ref-562" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-562"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>540<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>

### Visual arts

Main articles: [Visual art of the United States](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Visual_art_of_the_United_States "Visual art of the United States") and [Architecture of the United States](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Architecture_of_the_United_States "Architecture of the United States")

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Grant_Wood_-_American_Gothic_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/250px-Grant_Wood_-_American_Gothic_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg)

*[American Gothic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/American_Gothic "American Gothic")* (1930) by [Grant Wood](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Grant_Wood "Grant Wood") is one of the most famous [American paintings](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Visual_art_of_the_United_States "Visual art of the United States") and is widely [parodied](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Parody "Parody").<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_563-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-BBC-563"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>541<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>

[Folk art](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Folk_art_of_the_United_States "Folk art of the United States") in [colonial America](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Colonial_america "Colonial america") grew out of artisanal [craftsmanship](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Workmanship "Workmanship") in communities that allowed commonly trained people to individually express themselves. It was distinct from Europe's tradition of [high art](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/High_culture "High culture"), which was less accessible and generally less relevant to early American settlers.<sup id="cite_ref-564" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-564"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>542<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> Cultural movements in art and craftsmanship in colonial America generally lagged behind those of Western Europe. For example, the prevailing medieval style of [woodworking](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Woodworking "Woodworking") and primitive [sculpture](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sculpture_of_the_United_States "Sculpture of the United States") became integral to early American folk art, despite the emergence of [Renaissance styles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Renaissance_art "Renaissance art") in England in the late 16th and early 17th centuries. The new English styles would have been early enough to make a considerable impact on American folk art, but American styles and forms had already been firmly adopted. Not only did styles change slowly in early America, but there was a tendency for rural artisans there to continue their traditional forms longer than their urban counterparts did—and far longer than those in Western Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-Coleman-2013_516-2" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-Coleman-2013-516"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>494<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>

The [Hudson River School](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hudson_River_School "Hudson River School") was a mid-19th-century movement in the visual arts tradition of European [naturalism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Realism_\(arts\) "Realism (arts)"). The 1913 [Armory Show](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Armory_Show "Armory Show") in New York City, an exhibition of European [modernist art](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Modern_art "Modern art"), shocked the public and transformed the U.S. art scene.<sup id="cite_ref-565" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-565"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>543<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>

[American Realism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/American_Realism "American Realism") and [American Regionalism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/American_Regionalism "American Regionalism") sought to reflect and give America new ways of looking at itself. [Georgia O'Keeffe](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georgia_O%27Keeffe "Georgia O'Keeffe"), [Marsden Hartley](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Marsden_Hartley "Marsden Hartley"), and others experimented with new and individualistic styles, which would become known as [American modernism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/American_modernism "American modernism"). Major artistic movements such as the [abstract expressionism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abstract_expressionism "Abstract expressionism") of [Jackson Pollock](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jackson_Pollock "Jackson Pollock") and [Willem de Kooning](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Willem_de_Kooning "Willem de Kooning") and the [pop art](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pop_art "Pop art") of [Andy Warhol](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Andy_Warhol "Andy Warhol") and [Roy Lichtenstein](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roy_Lichtenstein "Roy Lichtenstein") developed largely in the United States. Major photographers include [Alfred Stieglitz](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alfred_Stieglitz "Alfred Stieglitz"), [Edward Steichen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Edward_Steichen "Edward Steichen"), [Dorothea Lange](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dorothea_Lange "Dorothea Lange"), [Edward Weston](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Edward_Weston "Edward Weston"), [James Van Der Zee](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/James_Van_Der_Zee "James Van Der Zee"), [Ansel Adams](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ansel_Adams "Ansel Adams"), and [Gordon Parks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gordon_Parks "Gordon Parks").<sup id="cite_ref-Davenport1991_566-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-Davenport1991-566"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>544<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>

The tide of [modernism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Modernism "Modernism") and then [postmodernism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Postmodernism "Postmodernism") has brought global fame to American architects, including [Frank Lloyd Wright](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Frank_Lloyd_Wright "Frank Lloyd Wright"), [Philip Johnson](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Philip_Johnson "Philip Johnson"), and [Frank Gehry](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Frank_Gehry "Frank Gehry").<sup id="cite_ref-JansonJanson2003_567-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-JansonJanson2003-567"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>545<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> The [Metropolitan Museum of Art](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Metropolitan_Museum_of_Art "Metropolitan Museum of Art") in [Manhattan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Manhattan "Manhattan") is the largest [art museum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Art_museum "Art museum") in the United States<sup id="cite_ref-METLargestArtMuseum_568-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-METLargestArtMuseum-568"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>546<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> and the [fourth-largest](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_largest_art_museums "List of largest art museums") in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-569" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-569"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>547<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>

### Music

Main article: [Music of the United States](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Music_of_the_United_States "Music of the United States")

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Country_music_hall_of_fame2.jpg/250px-Country_music_hall_of_fame2.jpg)

The [Country Music Hall of Fame and Museum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Country_Music_Hall_of_Fame_and_Museum "Country Music Hall of Fame and Museum") in [Nashville, Tennessee](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nashville,_Tennessee "Nashville, Tennessee")

[American folk music](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/American_folk_music "American folk music") encompasses numerous music genres, variously known as traditional music, traditional [folk music](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Folk_music "Folk music"), contemporary folk music, or roots music. Many traditional songs have been sung within the same family or folk group for generations, and sometimes trace back to such origins as the [British Isles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/British_Isles "British Isles"), [mainland Europe](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mainland_Europe "Mainland Europe"), or [Africa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/African-American_music "African-American music").<sup id="cite_ref-afc_570-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-afc-570"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>548<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> The rhythmic and lyrical styles of African-American music in particular have influenced American music.<sup id="cite_ref-571" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-571"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>549<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> [Banjos](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Banjo "Banjo") were brought to America through the slave trade. [Minstrel shows](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Minstrel_show "Minstrel show") incorporating the instrument into their acts led to its increased popularity and widespread production in the 19th century.<sup id="cite_ref-572" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-572"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>550<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShi2016378_573-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShi2016378-573"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>551<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> The [electric guitar](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electric_guitar "Electric guitar"), first invented in the 1930s, and mass-produced by the 1940s, had an enormous influence on popular music, in particular due to the development of [rock and roll](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rock_and_roll "Rock and roll").<sup id="cite_ref-axe_574-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-axe-574"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>552<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> The [synthesizer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Synthesizer "Synthesizer"), [turntablism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Turntablism "Turntablism"), and [electronic music](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electronic_music "Electronic music") were also largely developed in the U.S.

Elements from folk idioms such as the [blues](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Blues "Blues") and [old-time music](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Old-time_music "Old-time music") were adopted and transformed into [popular genres](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Popular_music "Popular music") with global audiences. [Jazz](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jazz "Jazz") grew from blues and [ragtime](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ragtime "Ragtime") in the early 20th century, developing from the innovations and recordings of composers such as [W.C. Handy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/W.C._Handy "W.C. Handy") and [Jelly Roll Morton](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jelly_Roll_Morton "Jelly Roll Morton"). [Louis Armstrong](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Louis_Armstrong "Louis Armstrong") and [Duke Ellington](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Duke_Ellington "Duke Ellington") increased its popularity early in the 20th century.<sup id="cite_ref-Biddle-2001_575-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-Biddle-2001-575"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>553<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> [Country music](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Country_music "Country music") developed in the 1920s,<sup id="cite_ref-576" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-576"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>554<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> rock and roll in the 1930s,<sup id="cite_ref-axe_574-1" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-axe-574"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>552<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> and [bluegrass](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bluegrass_music "Bluegrass music")<sup id="cite_ref-577" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#cite_note-577"><span
